import torch
import torch.nn as nn


class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Bootstrap particle filter (Sequential Monte Carlo) for a linear-
    Gaussian state-space model.  Implements the predict–weight–resample
    cycle for one time step.  The state transition is x_{t} = A x_{t-1} + w
    and the observation model is y_t = C x_t + v.
    """

    def __init__(self, state_dim, obs_dim, num_particles):
        super().__init__()
        self.state_dim = state_dim
        self.obs_dim = obs_dim
        self.num_particles = num_particles
        self.register_buffer("A", 0.9 * torch.eye(state_dim))
        self.register_buffer("C", torch.randn(obs_dim, state_dim) * 0.5)
        self.process_noise = 0.1
        self.obs_noise = 0.5

    def forward(
        self,
        particles: torch.Tensor,
        log_weights: torch.Tensor,
        observation: torch.Tensor,
    ) -> tuple:
        """
        Args:
            particles:   (B, P, state_dim)
            log_weights: (B, P)
            observation: (B, obs_dim)
        Returns:
            (new_particles, new_log_weights):
                new_particles:   (B, P, state_dim)
                new_log_weights: (B, P) – normalized log weights
        """
        B, P, D = particles.shape

        # Predict: propagate through dynamics
        pred = (particles @ self.A.t()) + self.process_noise * torch.randn_like(particles)

        # Weight: compute log p(y_t | x_t)
        obs_pred = pred @ self.C.t()  # (B, P, obs_dim)
        diff = observation.unsqueeze(1) - obs_pred
        log_lik = -0.5 * (diff ** 2).sum(-1) / (self.obs_noise ** 2)
        new_log_w = log_weights + log_lik
        # Normalize
        new_log_w = new_log_w - torch.logsumexp(new_log_w, dim=1, keepdim=True)

        # Systematic resample
        weights = torch.exp(new_log_w)
        cumsum = torch.cumsum(weights, dim=1)
        u = (torch.arange(P, device=particles.device, dtype=particles.dtype) + torch.rand(B, 1, device=particles.device)) / P
        indices = torch.searchsorted(cumsum, u)
        indices = indices.clamp(0, P - 1)

        batch_idx = torch.arange(B, device=particles.device).unsqueeze(1).expand(-1, P)
        resampled = pred[batch_idx, indices]
        uniform_log_w = torch.full_like(new_log_w, -torch.log(torch.tensor(float(P))))

        return resampled, uniform_log_w


state_dim = 4
obs_dim = 2
num_particles = 1024
batch_size = 16


def get_inputs():
    particles = torch.randn(batch_size, num_particles, state_dim)
    log_weights = torch.full((batch_size, num_particles), -torch.log(torch.tensor(float(num_particles))))
    observation = torch.randn(batch_size, obs_dim)
    return [particles, log_weights, observation]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [state_dim, obs_dim, num_particles]

So it's fixed. **Detailed kernel steps**: ``` int b = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; if (tid >= P) return; // offsets int particle_stride = P * D; int batch_offset = b * particle_stride; int weight_offset = b * P; // Step 1: predict float my_pred[4]; for (int d = 0; d < D; ++d) { float p = particles[batch_offset + tid * D + d]; float n = noise[batch_offset + tid * D + d]; float val = p * 0.9f + process_noise * n; my_pred[d] = val; s_pred[d * P + tid] = val; } __syncthreads(); // Step 2: observation prediction and log-likelihood float obs_pred[2]; #pragma unroll for (int m = 0; m < M; ++m) { float sum = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < D; ++d) { sum += my_pred[d] * C[m * D + d]; } obs_pred[m] = sum; } // load observation float lik = 0.0f; for (int m = 0; m < M; ++m) { float diff = observation[b * M + m] - obs_pred[m]; lik += diff * diff; } lik = -0.5f * lik / (obs_noise * obs_noise); float log_w = log_weights[weight_offset + tid] + lik; s_logw[tid] = log_w; __syncthreads(); // Step 3: logsumexp normalization // Parallel max reduction float local_max = log_w; for (int offset = blockDim.x / 2; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { if (tid < offset) { if (s_logw[tid + offset] > local_max) { local_max = s_logw[tid + offset]; } } __syncthreads(); } // wait, using shared memory for reduction is tricky. Better: // Use a shared mem scratch pad. ``` Store log_w in shared memory `s_logw[tid]`. Then do tree reduction for max: ``` float max_val = s_logw[tid]; for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (tid < stride) { float other = s_logw[tid + stride]; if (other > max_val) max_val = other; s_logw[tid] = max_val; } __syncthreads(); } ``` But this modifies s_logw. After reduction, s_logw[0] holds max. We need the max for all threads. Then compute sum of exp(logw - max). We can do: ``` if (tid == 0) { float max_logw = s_logw[0]; s_logw[0] = max_logw; } __syncthreads(); float max_logw = s_logw[0]; ``` Then compute `s_weights[tid] = expf(s_logw_original[tid] - max_logw)`. But s_logw was clobbered by reduction. So we need a separate array for weights. Or we can store original logw in a register `my_logw = log_w`. Then we can reuse s_logw for reduction. Better: ``` float my_logw = log_w; s_logw[tid] = my_logw; __syncthreads(); // max reduction using s_logw for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (tid < stride) { s_logw[tid] = fmaxf(s_logw[tid], s_logw[tid + stride]); } __syncthreads(); } float max_logw = s_logw[0]; __syncthreads(); // compute exp and sum s_weights[tid] = expf(my_logw - max_logw); __syncthreads(); // sum reduction on s_weights for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (tid < stride) { s_weights[tid] += s_weights[tid + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float sum_exp = s_weights[0]; __syncthreads(); float log_sum = max_logw + logf(sum_exp); float norm_logw = my_logw - log_sum; // Write output log_weights out_log_weights[weight_offset + tid] = norm_logw; // Prepare weights for cumsum: s_weights[tid] = expf(norm_logw); s_weights[tid] = expf(norm_logw); __syncthreads(); // Step 4: inclusive prefix sum on s_weights // Blelloch scan or Hillis-Steele? For P=1024, Hillis-Steele with P=1024 threads is easy but not work-efficient? It's okay. // Hillis-Steele inclusive scan: // In shared memory s_weights. for (int stride = 1; stride < P; stride *= 2) { float t = (tid >= stride) ? s_weights[tid - stride] : 0.0f; __syncthreads(); if (tid >= stride) s_weights[tid] += t; __syncthreads(); } ``` Wait, Hillis-Steele inclusive scan described above actually computes inclusive scan if we do: ``` for stride=1; stride<blockDim.x; stride*=2: if tid >= stride: s[tid] += s[tid - stride] __syncthreads() ``` Yes. The standard in-place Hillis-Steele: ``` for (int offset = 1; offset < blockDim.x; offset *= 2) { if (tid >= offset) { s_weights[tid] += s_weights[tid - offset]; } __syncthreads(); } ``` Does this compute inclusive prefix sum correctly? If it's in-place, thread 2 reads s[1] which is being written by thread 1 in same instruction. But within a warp, there is no guaranteed ordering. So we need a temporary register. Correct approach: ``` float val = s_weights[tid]; for (int offset = 1; offset < blockDim.x; offset *= 2) { float other = (tid >= offset) ? s_weights[tid - offset] : 0.0f; __syncthreads(); val += other; s_weights[tid] = val; __syncthreads(); } ``` But even this may have issues if `s_weights[tid - offset]` was updated in previous iteration. Actually we want it to be updated, because that's the point of scan. We need to use the value from the previous iteration. Then `other = s_weights[tid - offset]` reads the current shared memory. Then we sync after write. So: offset=1: read other = s[tid-1] (initial values). val = val + other. write s[tid] = val. s becomes: [a0, a0+a1, a1+a2, a2+a3]. This is not correct inclusive scan. The correct Hillis-Steele uses values that were just updated. Actually the algorithm is: ``` for d = 1 to log2(n): for i = 1 to n: if i >= 2^d: s[i] = s[i] + s[i - 2^{d-1}] (using the updated s from previous step) ``` Wait, the standard Hillis-Steele inclusive scan is: ``` for d = 0 to log2(n)-1: for all i in parallel: if i >= 2^d: out[i] = in[i] + in[i - 2^d] swap(in, out) ``` So you need two buffers, or you can do it in-place if you read into a temp before writing, but the source is the buffer from previous iteration. In place with `__syncthreads` between reads and writes works if each iteration reads from the shared memory (which holds the result of previous iteration) and writes to the same location. The key is that all reads happen before any writes. So: ``` float val = s_weights[tid]; __syncthreads(); // after initialization for (int offset = 1; offset < P; offset *= 2) { float addend = (tid >= offset) ? s_weights[tid - offset] : 0.0f; __syncthreads(); val += addend; s_weights[tid] = val; __syncthreads(); } ``` Trace: - init: s = [a0,a1,a2,a3]. `val` per thread = a[tid]. - offset=1: read addend: thread0:0, thread1:s[0]=a0, thread2:s[1]=a1, thread3:s[2]=a2. sync; write: s becomes [a0, a1+a0, a2+a1, a3+a2]. val same. - offset=2: read addend: thread0:0, thread1:0, thread2:s[0]=a0, thread3:s[1]=a0+a1. sync; write: s[2] += a0 => a2+a1+a0. s[3] += a0+a1 => a3+a2+a0+a1. s[0],s[1] unchanged. - Result: [a0, a0+a1, a0+a1+a2, a0+a1+a2+a3]. Let me draft the kernel: ```cuda __global__ void particle_filter_kernel( const float* particles, const float* log_weights, const float* observation, const float* A, const float* C, const float* noise, const float* rand_val, float process_noise, float obs_noise, int B, int P, int D, int O, float* out_particles, float* out_log_weights ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int p = threadIdx.x; extern __shared__ float s_mem[]; // Layout in shared memory float* s_log_w = s_mem; // P float* s_weights = s_mem; // P (alias) float* s_cumsum = s_mem; // P (alias) float* s_indices = s_mem + P; // P float* s_obs = s_mem + 2 * P; // O float* s_A = s_mem + 2 * P + O; // D*D float* s_C = s_mem + 2 * P + O + D * D; // O*D float* s_pred = s_mem + 2 * P + O + D * D + O * D; // P*D // Load A and C (first few threads) if (p < D * D) s_A[p] = A[p]; if (p < O * D) s_C[p] = C[p]; __syncthreads(); // Load observation (first O threads) if (p < O) s_obs[p] = observation[b * O + p]; __syncthreads(); // Compute prediction and likelihood float pred[4]; // D=4, unroll in registers // Load particle int base = b * P * D + p * D; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { pred[d] = particles[base + d]; } // pred = A^T * particle + noise #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < D; k++) { sum += s_A[k * D + d] * pred[k]; // A.t()[d, k] = A[k, d] } pred[d] = sum + noise[base + d]; } // obs_pred = C^T * pred float obs_pred[2]; #pragma unroll for (int o = 0; o < O; o++) { float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { sum += s_C[d * O + o] * pred[d]; // C.t()[o, d] = C[d, o] } obs_pred[o] = sum; } // diff = obs - obs_pred float diff[2]; float sq_diff = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int o = 0; o < O; o++) { diff[o] = s_obs[o] - obs_pred[o]; sq_diff += diff[o] * diff[o]; } float log_lik = -0.5f * sq_diff / (obs_noise * obs_noise); float lw = log_weights[b * P + p] + log_lik; s_log_w[p] = lw; // Store pred in shared memory for later resampling #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { s_pred[p * D + d] = pred[d]; } __syncthreads(); // Logsumexp reduction // Use shared memory reduction // ... standard parallel reduction on s_log_w // Track max and sum of exp(log_w - max) // Actually, let's do: find max, then sum of exp(log_w - max) // Step 1: max reduction for (int stride = P / 2; stride > 0; stride /= 2) { if (p < stride) { s_log_w[p] = fmaxf(s_log_w[p], s_log_w[p + stride]); } __syncthreads(); } float max_log_w = s_log_w[0]; __syncthreads(); // Step 2: compute exp(log_w - max) s_weights[p] = expf(lw - max_log_w); __syncthreads(); // Step 3: sum reduction for (int stride = P / 2; stride > 0; stride /= 2) { if (p < stride) { s_weights[p] += s_weights[p + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float sum_exp = s_weights[0]; __syncthreads(); // Normalize log weights float norm_log_w = lw - (max_log_w + logf(sum_exp)); s_log_w[p] = norm_log_w; // Compute weights = exp(norm_log_w) for cumsum float w = expf(norm_log_w); s_cumsum[p] = w; __syncthreads(); // Parallel prefix sum (Blelloch scan) // Up-sweep for (int stride = 1; stride < P; stride *= 2) { if (p < P / (2 * stride)) { int idx = (2 * p + 2) * stride - 1; s_cumsum[idx] += s_cumsum[idx - stride]; } __syncthreads(); } // Down-sweep if (p == 0) s_cumsum[P - 1] = 0.0f; // We want inclusive scan, so need different approach __syncthreads(); // Actually, let me do inclusive scan differently // ... } ``` Hmm, the inclusive prefix sum can be done more simply. Actually, for searchsorted I need the actual cumulative probabilities. Let me think about a simpler approach. For P=1024, I can do a simple warp-level scan first, then block-level. For P=1024, that's 10 iterations. Each iteration has a sync, so 20 __syncthreads(). And in each step, half the threads add values from decreasing offsets. Actually, the Kogge-Stone algorithm: ```cuda for (int offset = 1; offset < blockDim.x; offset *= 2) { float t = (threadIdx.x >= offset) ? s_data[threadIdx.x - offset] : 0; __syncthreads(); s_data[threadIdx.x] += t; __syncthreads(); } ``` This works and produces inclusive scan. For P=1024, it's 10 iterations, each with 2 syncs. That's fine. But wait, with weights summing to 1, the cumsum[P-1] should be 1. Due to floating point it might be slightly off, but that's fine. However, due to floating point, cumsum might not reach exactly 1, or the last element might be slightly less than 1. The reference does `indices.clamp(0, P - 1)` to handle edge cases. Let me fix the binary search: Actually, let me think about this more carefully. For `left < right` loop: - If cumsum[P-1] < u, then for every mid, cumsum[mid] < u, so left keeps increasing. - Eventually left = right = P-1. Now left < right is false, so we exit. - But we never checked cumsum[P-1] < u during the last iteration when left=right. Wait, let me trace more carefully. The binary search assumes there exists some index where cumsum[idx] >= u. If not, it returns the largest possible index, which might still have cumsum[idx] < u. In the reference, `torch.searchsorted(cumsum, u)` where cumsum[-1] < u would return P (i.e., len(cumsum)), and then the clamp brings it to P-1. In my binary search, if I clamp to [0, P-1], I get P-1. Actually, let me just use shared memory for simplicity: ```cuda // Max reduction __shared__ float s_max; float local_max = lw; s_log_w[p] = local_max; __syncthreads(); for (int stride = P / 2; stride > 0; stride /= 2) { if (p < stride) { s_log_w[p] = fmaxf(s_log_w[p], s_log_w[p + stride]); } __syncthreads(); } if (p == 0) s_max = s_log_w[0]; __syncthreads(); float max_val = s_max; // Compute exp(log_w - max) float exp_val = expf(lw - max_val); s_weights[p] = exp_val; __syncthreads(); // Sum reduction for (int stride = P / 2; stride > 0; stride /= 2) { if (p < stride) { s_weights[p] += s_weights[p + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } if (p == 0) s_sum = s_weights[0]; // need another shared variable __syncthreads(); float lse = s_max + logf(s_sum); float norm_lw = lw - lse; ``` This is straightforward. But with 3 rounds of __syncthreads() in each reduction, it's 3*10 = 30 barriers for the max reduction alone. Same for sum. Total ~60 barriers. Can I optimize this? Yes, using warp shuffles: ```cuda // Warp-level max reduction float max_val = lw; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { max_val = fmaxf(max_val, __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, max_val, offset)); } __shared__ float warp_max[32]; if (p % 32 == 0) warp_max[p / 32] = max_val; __syncthreads(); if (p < 32) { max_val = warp_max[p]; for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { max_val = fmaxf(max_val, __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, max_val, offset)); } if (p == 0) s_max = max_val; } __syncthreads(); max_val = s_max; ``` Then do the same for sum of exp values. Proof: - If s_weights[mid] < u, the answer must be > mid, so left = mid + 1. - If s_weights[mid] >= u, the answer is <= mid, so right = mid. - Loop invariant: answer is in [left, right]. - Terminates with left == right, which is the answer. I'll write a helper function that works for any P up to 1024. ```cpp __inline__ __device__ float warp_scan_inclusive(float val, int lane) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 1; offset < 32; offset *= 2) { float y = __shfl_up_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); if (lane >= offset) val += y; } return val; } __inline__ __device__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); } return val; } template <int D, int O> __global__ void pf_kernel(...) { int b = blockIdx.x; int p = threadIdx.x; int lane = p % 32; int warp_id = p / 32; if (p >= P) return; // handle extra threads // ... computation ... // Inclusive scan of weights float val = (p < P) ? w : 0.0f; float warp_sum = warp_scan_inclusive(val, lane); __shared__ float s_warp_sums[32]; if (lane == 31) s_warp_sums[warp_id] = warp_sum; __syncthreads(); // Exclusive scan of warp sums if (warp_id == 0) { float t = (lane < (P + 31) / 32) ? s_warp_sums[lane] : 0.0f; float incl = warp_scan_inclusive(t, lane); s_warp_sums[lane] = incl - t; } __syncthreads(); float prefix = (p < P) ? (val + s_warp_sums[warp_id]) : 0.0f; if (p < P) s_weights[p] = prefix; __syncthreads(); } ``` This works for any P. For P=1024, `(P+31)/32 = 32` warps. warp 0 handles all 32 warp sums. Wait, warp_scan_inclusive for the block scan: warp 0 has 32 threads. lane 0..31. But there are only 32 warp sums. So all 32 threads in warp 0 participate. If P is not a multiple of 32, some warps might have fewer active threads. For example, P=1000 means warp 31 has only 1000-992=8 active threads. The warp scan still works because inactive threads just don't add the shuffle result (lane >= offset check). But the warp sum for warp 31 would be the sum of all 32 threads' values. Here's the updated kernel: ```cpp template <int D, int O> __global__ void particle_filter_kernel( const float* __restrict__ particles, const float* __restrict__ log_weights, const float* __restrict__ observation, const float* __restrict__ A, const float* __restrict__ C, const float* __restrict__ noise, const float* __restrict__ rand_val, int B, int P, float obs_noise, float* __restrict__ out_particles, float* __restrict__ out_log_weights ) { int b = blockIdx.x; if (p < O) s_obs[p] = observation[b * O + p]; __syncthreads(); // Compute pred int pbase = (b * P + p) * D; float pred_reg[D]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) pred_reg[d] = particles[pbase + d]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < D; k++) sum += s_A[d * D + k] * pred_reg[k]; pred_reg[d] = sum + noise[pbase + d]; } #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) s_pred[p * D + d] = pred_reg[d]; // Compute log_lik float obs_pred_reg[O]; #pragma unroll for (int o = 0; o < O; o++) { float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) sum += s_C[o * D + d] * pred_reg[d]; obs_pred_reg[o] = sum; } float sq_diff = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int o = 0; o < O; o++) { float diff = s_obs[o] - obs_pred_reg[o]; sq_diff += diff * diff; } float log_lik = -0.5f * sq_diff / (obs_noise * obs_noise); float lw = log_weights[b * P + p] + log_lik; // Logsumexp float max_val = lw; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) max_val = fmaxf(max_val, __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, max_val, offset)); if (lane == 0) s_warp_buf[warp_id] = max_val; __syncthreads(); if (warp_id == 0) { max_val = (lane < (P + 31) / 32) ? s_warp_buf[lane] : -INFINITY; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) max_val = fmaxf(max_val, __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, max_val, offset)); if (lane == 0) s_scalar[0] = max_val; } __syncthreads(); max_val = s_scalar[0]; float exp_val = expf(lw - max_val); float sum_val = exp_val; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) sum_val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_val, offset); if (lane == 0) s_warp_buf[warp_id] = sum_val; __syncthreads(); if (warp_id == 0) { sum_val = (lane < (P + 31) / 32) ? s_warp_buf[lane] : 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) sum_val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_val, offset); if (lane == 0) s_scalar[1] = sum_val; } __syncthreads(); sum_val = s_scalar[1]; float norm_lw = lw - (max_val + logf(sum_val)); // Compute weights and store float w = expf(norm_lw); __syncthreads(); // Sequential cumsum by thread 0 for numerical exactness if (p == 0) { float acc = 0.0f; for (int i = 0; i < P; i++) { acc += w; // wait, w is thread p's weight, not thread i's weight // Problem: each thread has its own w in a register } } ``` Ah, I see the problem! After computing `norm_lw`, each thread has its own `w` in a register. To do sequential cumsum, I need all weights in shared memory. So I should write `w` to shared memory: ```cpp s_cumsum[p] = w; __syncthreads(); if (p == 0) { float acc = 0.0f; for (int i = 0; i < P; i++) { acc += s_cumsum[i]; s_cumsum[i] = acc; } } __syncthreads(); ``` This is clean. Thread 0 reads from shared memory sequentially and writes back. This exactly matches sequential scan. For P=1024, it's 1024 iterations of read-add-write. Each iteration is a few instructions. Should take ~5K cycles. At 1.5GHz, ~3 microseconds per block. For 16 blocks, if they all run concurrently, still ~3 microseconds total (if enough SMs). Even if serialized, ~50 microseconds. Very fast! Then binary search: ```cpp float u = (float(p) + rand_val[b]) / float(P); int left = 0, right = P - 1; while (left < right) { int mid = (left + right) >> 1; if (s_cumsum[mid] < u) left = mid + 1; else right = mid; } int idx = left; // Gather #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { out_particles[pbase + d] = s_pred[idx * D + d]; } out_log_weights[b * P + p] = -logf(float(P)); ``` Good. Now, one optimization: the loop `for (int i = 0; i < P; i++)` has a memory dependency between iterations (acc += s_cumsum[i]). But shared memory latency is low (~20 cycles), and the dependency chain is add + load. For simple float operations, it's limited by instruction latency. Each iteration is about 3-4 instructions with ~4 cycle latency each, so ~12-16 cycles per iteration. For 1024 iterations, that's ~16K cycles per block. Still about 10 microseconds. Fine. Could I use float2 or vector loads? Probably not necessary. Let me also handle the case where P might not equal 1024. I'll use `blockDim.x` for the number of threads, and require `blockDim.x >= P`. Actually, for simplicity and since P=1024 is fixed, I'll launch with exactly 1024 threads. But to be robust, I can use: ```python threads = P if P <= 1024 else 1024 # but then we'd need more complex code ``` For this problem, P=1024. I'll just assume blockDim.x >= P and threads >= P. Now let me write the complete code. One more concern: `logf(sum_val)` might have issues if `sum_val` is 0 or negative. But `sum_val` is the sum of positive values (exp of something), so it's always positive. If all weights are extremely tiny, sum_val could underflow to 0. But that's unlikely for P=1024 and float32. The reference would have the same issue. Also, `-logf(float(P))` for P=1024: logf(1024.0f) = 6.9314718f. This should be fine. Alright, let me write the final code. I need to: 1. Let me write the complete code. ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <c10/cuda/CUDAStream.h> template <int D, int O> __global__ void particle_filter_kernel( const float* __restrict__ particles, const float* __restrict__ log_weights, const float* __restrict__ observation, const float* __restrict__ A, const float* __restrict__ C, const float* __restrict__ noise, const float* __restrict__ rand_val, int B, int P, float obs_noise, float* __restrict__ out_particles, float* __restrict__ out_log_weights ) { int b = blockIdx.x; __syncthreads(); if (warp_id == 0) { sum_val = (lane < (P + 31) / 32) ? s_warp_buf[lane] : 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) sum_val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum_val, offset); auto out_log_weights = torch::empty_like(log_weights); int threads = P; size_t smem_size = sizeof(float) * (P * D + P + 32 + D * D + O * D + O + 2); dim3 blocks(B); dim3 block_size(threads); auto stream = at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream(); if (D == 4 && O == 2) { particle_filter_kernel<4, 2><<<blocks, block_size, smem_size, stream>>>( particles.data_ptr<float>(), log_weights.data_ptr<float>(), observation.data_ptr<float>(), A.data_ptr<float>(), C.data_ptr<float>(), noise.data_ptr<float>(), rand_val.data_ptr<float>(), B, P, obs_noise, out_particles.data_ptr<float>(), out_log_weights.data_ptr<float>() ); } else { AT_ERROR("Unsupported state_dim/obs_dim combination"); } C10_CUDA_KERNEL_LAUNCH_CHECK(); return std::make_tuple(out_particles, out_log_weights); } """ _C = load_inline( name="particle_filter", cpp_sources="", cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["particle_filter_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, state_dim, obs_dim, num_particles): super().__init__() self.state_dim = state_dim self.obs_dim = obs_dim self.num_particles = num_particles self.register_buffer("A", 0.9 * torch.eye(state_dim)) self.register_buffer("C", torch.randn(obs_dim, state_dim) * 0.5) self.process_noise = 0.1 self.obs_noise = 0.5 def forward( self, particles: torch.Tensor, log_weights: torch.Tensor, observation: torch.Tensor, ) -> tuple: B, P, D = particles.shape O = self.obs_dim device = particles.device noise = self.process_noise * torch.randn_like(particles) rand_val = torch.rand(B, 1, device=device, dtype=particles.dtype) out_particles, out_log_weights = _C.particle_filter_forward( particles, log_weights, observation, self.A, self.C, noise, rand_val, B, P, D, O, self.obs_noise ) return out_particles, out_log_weights ``` Wait, I should check whether `load_inline` imports `torch` and `at` automatically. The max_difference is quite high (around 1.57), but the average difference is very low (around 0.0001). This suggests that only a small fraction of elements are different. Let me think about what might cause this. The issue might be with the search logic or the resampling indices. Let me trace through the searchsorted-like logic: In the reference: ```python u = (torch.arange(P, device=particles.device, dtype=particles.dtype) + torch.rand(B, 1, device=particles.device)) / P indices = torch.searchsorted(cumsum, u) indices = indices.clamp(0, P - 1) ``` In my CUDA code: ```c float u = (float(p) + rand_val[b]) / float(P); int left = 0; int right = P - 1; while (left < right) { int mid = (left + right) >> 1; if (s_cumsum[mid] < u) left = mid + 1; else right = mid; } int idx = left; ``` Wait, `torch.searchsorted` with default mode ('left') returns the first index i such that cumsum[i] >= u. Actually let me check my binary search more carefully: - mid = (left + right) / 2 - if s_cumsum[mid] < u: left = mid + 1 (u is beyond mid) - else: right = mid (u is at or before mid) This should return the first index where s_cumsum[i] >= u. That's the same as `torch.searchsorted(cumsum, u, side='left')`. But wait - there's a potential issue with floating point. When s_cumsum[P-1] < u due to floating point precision issues, the binary search in the reference is handled by clamping to P-1. In my binary search, when u > s_cumsum[P-1], the binary search would still return P-1 because when left = right = P-1, the while loop stops and idx = P-1. But wait... Actually, if u > s_cumsum[P-1], then during the search, the condition `s_cumsum[mid] < u` is always true since all values are < u. So left keeps increasing until left = mid + 1. When left = P-1, right = P-1. Then left < right is false (P-1 < P-1 is false), so idx = left = P-1. Hmm, but what if somehow the loop doesn't terminate? No, it should always terminate. Wait, maybe the issue is that in the reference `searchsorted` has a slightly different behavior for edge cases. Let me double check with an example. Actually, let me think about this differently. There's also the issue of `rand_val` vs `torch.rand(B, 1)`. The reference adds `torch.rand(B, 1)` to each value. In our code, we pass a precomputed `rand_val` as `torch.rand(B, 1)`. But the main correctness issue with high max_difference but very low average difference - this could be caused by the binary search giving slightly different results for a few elements due to floating point differences. Actually, I just realized: in the reference code, the `norm_lw` uses `torch.logsumexp`, which is numerically stable. In our CUDA code, we compute: ```c float norm_lw = lw - (max_val + logf(sum_val)); ``` The issue is: `lw - max_val - logf(sum_val)` can lose precision. Also, the `logf(sum_val)` vs `log(sum_val)` difference could cause issues. Actually, the more likely issue is in the cumsum. The reference `torch.cumsum` runs on weights which are computed from normalized log weights. The cumsum values can be slightly different due to floating point order-of-operations differences. With binary search, this can lead to different indices for elements near the boundaries. Let me think about how to fix this. One approach is to clamp the binary search result to [0, P-1] as the reference does with `indices.clamp(0, P - 1)`. Our binary search already naturally gives indices in [0, P-1]. Actually wait, `torch.searchsorted(cumsum, u)` with default `right=False` (side='left') returns: "i such that cumsum[i-1] < u <= cumsum[i]", or more precisely, "the first index i such that cumsum[i] >= u". Hmm, let me look at the exact semantics. For `torch.searchsorted(sorted_sequence, values, right=False)`: - For 1D sorted_sequence, returns the first index i such that sorted_sequence[i] >= values[j] for each j. - If all elements are < values[j], returns len(sorted_sequence). Wait, that's for 1D `sorted_sequence`. For batched `searchsorted`... In the reference, cumsum has shape (B, P) and u has shape (B, P). So `torch.searchsorted(cumsum, u)` would work on the last dimension. It returns the last dimension index. Actually, looking at the PyTorch docs for `searchsorted`: for 2D sorted_sequence of shape (B, P) and 2D values of shape (B, P), it returns indices of shape (B, P), where for each batch b, it finds the index in sorted_sequence[b] such that sorted_sequence[b][i] >= values[b][j]. Now, for binary search, there's a crucial detail. When the array is sorted but with duplicate values, `searchsorted` with side='left' returns the first index where the value is >= the target. In my binary search, I have: ```c if (s_cumsum[mid] < u) left = mid + 1; else right = mid; ``` This finds the leftmost index where s_cumsum[i] >= u. That should be correct. Wait, there could be an issue with how I handle shared memory when storing s_cumsum. The cumsum is written sequentially by thread 0 and then other threads read from it. Since we're doing `__syncthreads()` after both the write and before the read, that should be fine. One more thought: the reference code uses `torch.exp(new_log_w)` to get weights. The cumsum is computed on these weights. In our CUDA code, we compute `expf(norm_lw)` where `norm_lw` is in log space. Due to floating point differences in log-sum-exp computation, the resulting weights might differ slightly. Actually, let me look at the logsumexp computation more carefully. In the reference: ```python new_log_w = new_log_w - torch.logsumexp(new_log_w, dim=1, keepdim=True) ``` In our CUDA code: ```c float norm_lw = lw - (max_val + logf(sum_val)); ``` `torch.logsumexp` computes `max + log(sum(exp(x - max)))`. In our code, we compute `max_val + logf(sum_val)` where `sum_val = sum(exp(lw - max_val))`. So `max_val + logf(sum_val)` = logsumexp. The difference is in the precision of `logf`. Wait! `logf` is `float` (32-bit) precision. But `torch.logsumexp` might use higher precision internally. That shouldn't matter much for fp32 output. Actually, let me reconsider the problem. The outputs are: - new_particles (B, P, D) - new_log_weights (B, P) The max_difference is up to 1.82 but avg_difference is only 0.0001. This pattern strongly suggests off-by-one indices in resampling for a small fraction of particles. Let me look at the binary search boundary case more carefully. If u == s_cumsum[i] for some i, the reference `searchsorted` with side='left' returns the first index where the cumsum >= u. If there are duplicate values in the cumsum (which can happen with very small weights), `searchsorted` returns the first index. Actually, looking at the data flow again: the reference code computes `u` as `(arange(P) + rand(B, 1)) / P`. This gives values in [0, 1). The cumsum of weights (which sum to 1.0) is in [0, 1]. Due to floating point, the last element of cumsum might be slightly less than 1.0. The reference then does `torch.searchsorted(cumsum, u)` which can return P for elements where u > last cumsum value. Actually, one more optimization: for D=4 and O=2, the matmuls are tiny. The dominant cost is kernel launch overhead and memory bandwidth, not compute. By fusing everything into one kernel, we read particles once, do all compute in registers, and write pred and log_weights once. For each particle: - Read: 4 floats (particles) + 1 float (log_weights) - Read shared: 16 floats (A) + 8 floats (C) + 2 floats (observation per batch) - cached in shared mem or registers per block - Read: 4 floats (noise) - Write: 4 floats (pred) + 1 float (raw_log_w) Memory bandwidth: ~14 floats per particle = 56 bytes. Total: 16 * 1024 * 56 = ~917KB. This is tiny and CPU-limited by kernel launch, not memory. Let me implement this now. The kernel will be straightforward. Actually, should I use shared memory for A and C? For a block per batch, yes, to avoid repeated global memory reads. Each thread in the block can load part of A and C. But since A is only 16 floats and C is 8 floats, and we're reading them from global memory, the cost is negligible. The threads can just read directly from global memory - it will be cached in L1. Even simpler: I'll declare A as `const float* __restrict__ A` and read directly. The compiler will likely keep the small array in registers. Let me write the CUDA kernel: ```cpp __global__ void compute_pred_weights( const float* __restrict__ particles, const float* __restrict__ log_weights, const float* __restrict__ observation, const float* __restrict__ A, const float* __restrict__ C, const float* __restrict__ noise, int B, int P, int D, int O, float obs_noise, float* __restrict__ pred, float* __restrict__ raw_log_w ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= B * P) return; int b = idx / P; int p = idx % P; // Load A into registers (D*D elements, small enough) float a[4][4]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < D; i++) #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < D; j++) a[i][j] = A[i * D + j]; // Load C into registers (O*D elements) float c[2][4]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < O; i++) #pragma unroll for (int j = 0; j < D; j++) c[i][j] = C[i * D + j]; // Load observation for this batch float obs[2]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < O; i++) obs[i] = observation[b * O + i]; // Load particle float pvec[4]; int pbase = idx * D; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) pvec[d] = particles[pbase + d]; // Compute pred = A @ pvec + noise float presult[4]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < D; k++) sum += a[d][k] * pvec[k]; presult[d] = sum + noise[pbase + d]; } // Store pred #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) pred[pbase + d] = presult[d]; // Compute obs_pred = C @ presult float op[2]; #pragma unroll for (int o = 0; o < O; o++) { float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) sum += c[o][d] * presult[d]; op[o] = sum; } // Compute log_lik float sq_diff = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int o = 0; o < O; o++) { float diff = obs[o] - op[o]; sq_diff += diff * diff; } float log_lik = -0.5f * sq_diff / (obs_noise * obs_noise); raw_log_w[idx] = log_weights[idx] + log_lik; } ``` This is a simple kernel. It's not exploiting the fact that A is diagonal, but for correctness, we should match PyTorch exactly. And PyTorch does `particles @ A.t()` generically. Wait, does `particles @ A.t()` with PyTorch for a 3D tensor and 2D matrix compute `particles[b, p, :] @ A.t()` for each b, p? Yes. And for A being diagonal, it computes `sum_k particles[b, p, k] * A.t()[k, d]` = `sum_k particles[b, p, k] * A[d, k]`. For A = 0.9 * eye(4), A[d, k] = 0.9 when d == k, else 0. So the sum reduces to `particles[b, p, d] * 0.9`. But PyTorch doesn't know this and computes the full sum. My kernel also computes the full sum. So they should match. But wait: PyTorch's matmul for 3D x 2D might optimize differently. Let me check if PyTorch does `bmm` or something else. For tensor operation `(B, P, D) @ (D, D)`, PyTorch treats the last two dimensions as matrices and broadcasts over the batch. So it computes `(B*P, D) @ (D, D)` effectively. The cuBLAS/ATen implementation might do this very efficiently. Given that P=1024 and D=4, this is a small problem size. The custom kernel might not be much faster than PyTorch's optimized path. But the key advantage is fusing multiple operations together. Let me also think about how many FLOPs this is: - For each particle: - Matmul with A: 4 * 4 = 16 mults + 12 adds = 28 FLOPs - Matmul with C: 2 * 4 = 8 mults + 6 adds = 14 FLOPs - Diff, square, sum, scaling: ~10 FLOPs - Total: ~52 FLOPs per particle - For B=16, P=1024: ~850K FLOPs This is a tiny amount of compute. The custom kernel saves on kernel launch overhead, not compute. With ~10 kernel launches in the reference, each taking ~5-10 microseconds of overhead, the fused kernel could save 50-100 microseconds. Hmm, but is 50-100 microseconds significant compared to the total runtime? The total reference runtime might be around 100-200 microseconds. So 50-100 microseconds could be a 2x speedup! OK, let me also try to keep everything in a single kernel but only output pred and raw_log_w. Then in Python: ```python new_log_w = raw_log_w - torch.logsumexp(raw_log_w, dim=1, keepdim=True) weights = torch.exp(new_log_w) cumsum = torch.cumsum(weights, dim=1) u = (torch.arange(P, device=device, dtype=particles.dtype) + torch.rand(B, 1, device=device, dtype=particles.dtype)) / P indices = torch.searchsorted(cumsum, u) indices = indices.clamp(0, P - 1) batch_idx = torch.arange(B, device=device).unsqueeze(1).expand(-1, P) resampled = pred[batch_idx, indices] uniform_log_w = torch.full_like(new_log_w, -torch.log(torch.tensor(float(P), device=device))) return resampled, uniform_log_w ``` This is essentially the same as the reference but with pred and raw_log_w precomputed in the kernel. But wait, the reference also stores intermediate tensors. In this code, we only store `pred` and `raw_log_w`. Let me verify: in the reference: ```python pred = (particles @ self.A.t()) + self.process_noise * torch.randn_like(particles) obs_pred = pred @ self.C.t() diff = observation.unsqueeze(1) - obs_pred log_lik = -0.5 * (diff ** 2).sum(-1) / (self.obs_noise ** 2) new_log_w = log_weights + log_lik new_log_w = new_log_w - torch.logsumexp(new_log_w, dim=1, keepdim=True) ``` PyTorch creates tensors for `obs_pred`, `diff`, and `log_lik`. These are intermediate allocations. In my approach, the kernel computes all of these but only writes `pred` and `raw_log_w` to global memory. The intermediate values stay in registers. So we save ~3 tensor allocations and corresponding memory bandwidth. Let me write the kernel with shared memory: ```cpp __global__ void compute_pred_weights( const float* __restrict__ particles, const float* __restrict__ log_weights, const float* __restrict__ observation, const float* __restrict__ A, const float* __restrict__ C, const float* __restrict__ noise, int B, int P, int D, int O, float obs_noise, float* __restrict__ pred, float* __restrict__ raw_log_w ) { int b = blockIdx.x; int p = threadIdx.x; if (b >= B || p >= P) return; extern __shared__ float s_mem[]; float* s_A = s_mem; float* s_C = s_A + D * D; float* s_obs = s_C + O * D; if (p < D * D) s_A[p] = A[p]; if (p < O * D) s_C[p] = C[p]; if (p < O) s_obs[p] = observation[b * O + p]; __syncthreads(); int idx = b * P + p; int pbase = idx * D; float pvec[4]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) pvec[d] = particles[pbase + d]; float presult[4]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < D; k++) sum += s_A[d * D + k] * pvec[k]; presult[d] = sum + noise[pbase + d]; } #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) pred[pbase + d] = presult[d]; float op[2]; #pragma unroll for (int o = 0; o < O; o++) { float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) sum += s_C[o * D + d] * presult[d]; op[o] = sum; } float sq_diff = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int o = 0; o < O; o++) { float diff = s_obs[o] - op[o]; sq_diff += diff * diff; } float log_lik = -0.5f * sq_diff / (obs_noise * obs_noise); raw_log_w[idx] = log_weights[idx] + log_lik; } ``` Shared memory size: D*D + O*D + O = 16 + 8 + 2 = 26 floats = 104 bytes. OK here's the code: ```python import os os.environ['LD_LIBRARY_PATH'] = '/opt/conda/lib:' + os.environ.get('LD_LIBRARY_PATH', '') import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cpp_sources = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> std::tuple<at::Tensor, at::Tensor> compute_pred_and_weights( at::Tensor particles, at::Tensor log_weights, at::Tensor observation, at::Tensor A, at::Tensor C, at::Tensor noise, int B, int P, int D, int O, float obs_noise ); int p = threadIdx.x; int lane = p & 31; int warp_id = p >> 5; if (b >= B || p >= P) return; extern __shared__ float s_mem[]; float* s_A = s_mem; float* s_C = s_A + D * D; float* s_obs = s_C + O * D; if (p < D * D) s_A[p] = A[p]; if (p < O * D) s_C[p] = C[p]; if (p < O) s_obs[p] = observation[b * O + p]; __syncthreads(); int idx = b * P + p; int pbase = idx * D; float pvec[4]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) pvec[d] = particles[pbase + d]; float presult[4]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int k = 0; k < D; k++) sum += s_A[d * D + k] * pvec[k]; presult[d] = sum + noise[pbase + d]; } #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) pred[pbase + d] = presult[d]; float op[2]; #pragma unroll for (int o = 0; o < O; o++) { float sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) sum += s_C[o * D + d] * presult[d]; op[o] = sum; } float sq_diff = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int o = 0; o < O; o++) { float diff = s_obs[o] - op[o]; sq_diff += diff * diff; } float log_lik = -0.5f * sq_diff / (obs_noise * obs_noise); raw_log_w[idx] = log_weights[idx] + log_lik; } std::tuple<at::Tensor, at::Tensor> compute_pred_and_weights( at::Tensor particles, at::Tensor log_weights, at::Tensor observation, at::Tensor A, at::Tensor C, at::Tensor noise, int B, int P, int D, int O, float obs_noise ) { TORCH_CHECK(P <= 1024, "num_particles must be <= 1024"); TORCH_CHECK(P % 32 == 0, "num_particles must be a multiple of 32"); auto pred = torch::empty_like(particles); auto raw_log_w = torch::empty({B, P}, log_weights.options()); int threads = P; size_t smem_size = sizeof(float) * (D * D + O * D + O); auto stream = at::cuda::getCurrentCUDAStream(); compute_pred_and_weights_kernel<<<B, threads, smem_size, stream>>>( particles.data_ptr<float>(), log_weights.data_ptr<float>(), observation.data_ptr<float>(), A.data_ptr<float>(), C.data_ptr<float>(), noise.data_ptr<float>(), B, P, D, O, obs_noise, pred.data_ptr<float>(), raw_log_w.data_ptr<float>() ); C10_CUDA_KERNEL_LAUNCH_CHECK(); return std::make_tuple(pred, raw_log_w); } """ _C = load_inline( name="pf_pred_weights", cpp_sources=cpp_sources, cuda_sources=cuda_sources, functions=["compute_pred_and_weights"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], extra_ldflags=["-static-libstdc++"], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, state_dim, obs_dim, num_particles): super().__init__() self.state_dim = state_dim self.obs_dim = obs_dim self.num_particles = num_particles self.register_buffer("A", 0.9 * torch.eye(state_dim)) self.register_buffer("C", torch.randn(obs_dim, state_dim) * 0.5) self.process_noise = 0.1 self.obs_noise = 0.5 def forward(self, particles, log_weights, observation): B, P, D = particles.shape O = self.obs_dim noise = self.process_noise * torch.randn_like(particles) pred, raw_log_w = _C.compute_pred_and_weights( particles.contiguous(), log_weights.contiguous(), observation.contiguous(), self.A.contiguous(), self.C.contiguous(), noise.contiguous(), B, P, D, O, self.obs_noise ) new_log_w = raw_log_w - torch.logsumexp(raw_log_w, dim=1, keepdim=True) weights = torch.exp(new_log_w) cumsum = torch.cumsum(weights, dim=1) u = (torch.arange(P, device=particles.device, dtype=particles.dtype) + torch.rand(B, 1, device=particles.device, dtype=particles.dtype)) / P indices = torch.searchsorted(cumsum, u) indices = indices.clamp(0, P - 1) batch_idx = torch.arange(B, device=particles.device).unsqueeze(1).expand(-1, P) resampled = pred[batch_idx, indices] uniform_log_w = torch.full_like(new_log_w, -torch.log(torch.tensor(float(P), device=particles.device))) return resampled, uniform_log_w ``` Wait, I should verify: does `self.A.contiguous()` give the same layout? Yes, for a 2D tensor, contiguous() gives row-major layout, which matches our kernel's `A[d * D + k]` access. One more optimization: I could compute `obs_noise * obs_noise` once outside the kernel and pass it as a parameter. I already pass `obs_noise` and compute `obs_noise * obs_noise` inside. Actually, PyTorch computes `self.obs_noise ** 2` as 0.25 once and reuses it. In my kernel, I compute `obs_noise * obs_noise` in each thread. Since `obs_noise` is passed as a scalar argument and stored in a register, `obs_noise * obs_noise` is a single register multiplication per thread. It's negligible. But to exactly match, I could pass `obs_noise_sq` instead. Actually, I want to match `self.obs_noise ** 2` exactly. In Python, `0.5 ** 2 = 0.25` with double precision. When cast to float32, it's exactly 0.25. In CUDA, `0.5f * 0.5f = 0.25f`, also exact. So passing either `obs_noise` or `obs_noise_sq` should give the same result. Let me keep passing `obs_noise` as the reference does. One more concern: `torch.randn_like(particles)` generates random noise. In the reference, this is added to the matmul result. In my model, I generate noise the same way and pass it to the kernel. So the noise is identical. Alright, let me write this up and test. Let me also think about whether there might be correctness issues with the shared memory indexing. In the kernel: - `s_A` has size D*D = 16 floats - `s_C` has size O*D = 8 floats - `s_obs` has size O = 2 floats - Total: 26 floats = 104 bytes For thread p < 16, it writes to s_A[p]. For thread p < 8, it writes to s_C[p]. For thread p < 2, it writes to s_obs[p]. Then __syncthreads() ensures all writes are visible. Then threads read s_A[d*D+k], s_C[o*D+d], and s_obs[o]. This should be correct. Wait, there's a potential issue: when D=4, s_A has 16 elements, s_C has 8 elements. But the threads write to s_A[p] for p < 16. Since p ranges from 0 to 1023, only threads 0-15 write to s_A. Similarly, threads 0-7 write to s_C, and threads 0-1 write to s_obs. Then __syncthreads() makes sure all writes are complete before reads. This is correct. But what if the shared memory isn't zero-initialized and threads read uninitialized values? No, because all threads in the block will have written to shared memory before __syncthreads(). Specifically, threads 0-15 write to s_A[0-15], so all of s_A is initialized. Threads 0-7 write to s_C[0-7], so all of s_C is initialized. Threads 0-1 write to s_obs[0-1], so all of s_obs is initialized. Then __syncthreads() ensures visibility. But wait, what if a block has fewer than 16 threads? No, our block size is P = 1024, which is > 16. One more potential issue: bank conflicts. With D=4, s_A is accessed as s_A[d*D + k] where d and k range from 0 to 3. If multiple threads access the same shared memory location, they broadcast (no bank conflict on modern GPUs for same-address reads). For different addresses, since D=4, consecutive threads accessing s_A[d*D + k] for the same d but different k would access banks k. With 4-byte floats and 32 banks, addresses 0, 4, 8, 12 map to banks 0, 4, 8, 12. No conflicts since these are all different banks. Actually, threads in a warp might access different combinations of d and k, but for a fixed d and varying k, there's no conflict as long as k values differ. Actually, in my code, each thread computes: ```c for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { for (int k = 0; k < D; k++) { sum += s_A[d * D + k] * pvec[k]; } } ``` For each d, all threads in a warp read s_A[d*D + 0], s_A[d*D + 1], etc. These are the same addresses for all threads in the warp. On modern GPUs, same-address reads from shared memory are broadcast, so there's no bank conflict. The pvec[k] values are thread-local (in registers). So shared memory access is efficient.
